9.1.3 Sorting/Supply at Filler and Closing (or Partial-Closing) Stations
Bulk components like plastic containers (such as droptainers for ophthalmics) and elastomers are supplied to
the filling line in large volume to reduce the frequency of replenishment. Regardless of the type of
container–closure and the number of stations required for their insertion, the use of bulk-supplied
components commonly requires a sorting device(s) that orients the component in the correct position for
filling or insertion. In addition, a bulk hopper may periodically feed components to the sorting station to
avoid frequent human intervention. Consideration should be given to the needed size of the bulk hoppers to
plan for the overall size of the batch and the number of packages of components that will be required. The
hopper may be sized to limit the number of times the replenishment operation will be required.
Although feeding by gravity is preferable, gravity-feeding is commonly augmented with vibratory functions
to ensure that the components flow easily from the hopper to the sorting station. The sorting station may
also make use of vibratory feeding to assist with separation and orientation functions. Vibration, gravity,
and/or vacuum may also be used at the point of insertion. Care should be taken to measure and control the
particulate at these important equipment locations as the vibration and friction of moving components can
generate or loosen particulate. In addition, care should be taken to ensure that the supply, sorting,
orientation, and insertion mechanisms do not damage components in any way. Handling and point-of-
insertion delivery (as well as all intended pretreatment steps) should be demonstrated during validation, as
closure damage may result in the loss of performance of sealing surfaces or loss of performance of
components, such as plungers.
Specialty preparations, such as some suspensions, may require glass or stainless-steel beads to be inserted
within the primary container. While the beads are not closures, the operations required for the insertion of
these components are similar in that they typically include vibratory feed, gravity, and vacuum. Therefore,
the same considerations as those described for supply and feeding of closures would apply.
Airflow studies within the Grade A environment should demonstrate controlled unidirectional airflow
during the intermittent (or continuous) vibratory episodes at the equipment locations. This requirement
would also apply to other locations that are subject to accumulation (e.g., turntables, bi-flow tables, loader
unloaders) that might result in intermittent container-on-container contact and generate particulate.

#### 9.1.4 Crimp Seals and Caps

Sterilized crimp seals and caps are required in isolators where the capper is within the Grade A environment
of the isolator. Sterilized crimp seals and caps are not mandatory for most conventional operations (e.g.,
RABS), but can be prepared as either sterile or nonsterile, according to the site CCS. When upgraded to a
sterile state, the intent is to provide additional protection of the closure and capping environment.
For applications that make use of RTPs (e.g., isolators, some RABS), caps can be fed into a polymer or
stainless-steel transport container (with or without sterility). Special care must be taken to prevent damage
or deformation of the seals/caps when handling them in bulk; therefore, a RTP with a soft-sided transfer bag
is not typically recommended.

#### 9.1.5 Considerations for Filters

For certain operations (e.g., nitrogen-gassing, vacuum-filling, product sterile-filtration), filter cartridges
might need to be transferred into the barrier (see Section 11.0). There are different ways to introduce filters:

--- PDF Page 174 ---
• Transfer via RTP container as a preassembled autoclaved configuration
• Transfer via RTP container/bag for aseptic installation within the barrier
• Transfer via RTP as a preassembled, presterilized, gamma-irradiated system
• Transfer of the autoclaved filter enclosed within a SteriBag as an autoclaved, preassembled
configuration
• Transfer of the autoclaved filter enclosed within a SteriBag for aseptic installation within the barrier
Filters can also be installed as nonsterile components during line setup but then would need to undergo a
CIP/SIP cycle and external biodecontamination after installation before use.

--- PDF Page 175 ---

## 10.0 Material Transfer System Design

Section 9.0 addressed the various methods of preparing and handling materials and components that are
needed to supply the aseptic filling line. Section 10.0 addresses the mechanistic aspects of material transfer.
When considering the mechanisms to introduce sterile or depyrogenated components to a filling system,
they can be divided into two categories of transfer: conveyance or docking. This section addresses both
categories as well as the considerations for decontamination based on the method of introduction.

### 10.1 Conveyance for Material Transfer

Conveyors are the traditional mechanism of movement through a filling system. Conveyors permit the direct
connection from one piece of equipment to another. An example of this is a tunnel conveyor exit across a
dead plate to the filling-system accumulator-table or filling conveyor. The NTT systems described in
Section 9.0, is an automatic or semiautomatic system for outer-bag and inner-bag removal that introduces a
presterilized tub or tray within the barrier, that would generally fall within the conveyor category since, after
the robotic entry, the tub or tray may be subjected to conventional conveyance or to complete robotic
movement. Conveyance systems are typically used for components.

### 10.2 Docking for Material Transfer

Docking is the process of connecting a bag, cannister, or other transport element (e.g., transport isolator) to a
RABS or isolator wall. The direct connection of equipment discharge-doors, such as autoclaves, ovens, or
lyophilizers (i.e., equipment with fixed-load positions) through the walls of a RABS or isolator also fall into
this category. Both the direct-equipment connection and the canister-connection commonly require an
isolator or RABS glove with a tool or a robotic system to perform an unloading or discharge intervention,
even if the door opening is automated.
Reducing the number of manual interventions is an important step in reducing the contamination risk for all
these points of entry. Therefore, when designing these docking interfaces, consideration should be given to
the end-to-end mechanisms of transfer for each component or equipment element, especially for critical
material transfers. In particular, as these docking systems often deliver critical-product-contact or indirect-
product-contact materials, consideration should be given to ensuring all materials and equipment in the
vicinity of, and to be removed from, the docking port are protected from contamination. For example, when
using a RTP to deliver stoppers or caps to feed bowls located in front of and below the port, avoiding
disruption of unidirectional airflow can be extremely difficult.
The diversity of transferred components and equipment may drive whether automating all docking and
unloading functions is possible. When it is not possible, glove ports are typically employed to perform the
material transfers. When operations are performed manually using isolator or RABS gloves, it is necessary
to ensure that the glove location and length is suitable for the operations to be carried out. In the case of

--- PDF Page 175 ---

## 10.0 Material Transfer System Design

Section 9.0 addressed the various methods of preparing and handling materials and components that are
needed to supply the aseptic filling line. Section 10.0 addresses the mechanistic aspects of material transfer.
When considering the mechanisms to introduce sterile or depyrogenated components to a filling system,
they can be divided into two categories of transfer: conveyance or docking. This section addresses both
categories as well as the considerations for decontamination based on the method of introduction.

### 10.1 Conveyance for Material Transfer

Conveyors are the traditional mechanism of movement through a filling system. Conveyors permit the direct
connection from one piece of equipment to another. An example of this is a tunnel conveyor exit across a
dead plate to the filling-system accumulator-table or filling conveyor. The NTT systems described in
Section 9.0, is an automatic or semiautomatic system for outer-bag and inner-bag removal that introduces a
presterilized tub or tray within the barrier, that would generally fall within the conveyor category since, after
the robotic entry, the tub or tray may be subjected to conventional conveyance or to complete robotic
movement. Conveyance systems are typically used for components.

### 10.2 Docking for Material Transfer

Docking is the process of connecting a bag, cannister, or other transport element (e.g., transport isolator) to a
RABS or isolator wall. The direct connection of equipment discharge-doors, such as autoclaves, ovens, or
lyophilizers (i.e., equipment with fixed-load positions) through the walls of a RABS or isolator also fall into
this category. Both the direct-equipment connection and the canister-connection commonly require an
isolator or RABS glove with a tool or a robotic system to perform an unloading or discharge intervention,
even if the door opening is automated.
Reducing the number of manual interventions is an important step in reducing the contamination risk for all
these points of entry. Therefore, when designing these docking interfaces, consideration should be given to
the end-to-end mechanisms of transfer for each component or equipment element, especially for critical
material transfers. In particular, as these docking systems often deliver critical-product-contact or indirect-
product-contact materials, consideration should be given to ensuring all materials and equipment in the
vicinity of, and to be removed from, the docking port are protected from contamination. For example, when
using a RTP to deliver stoppers or caps to feed bowls located in front of and below the port, avoiding
disruption of unidirectional airflow can be extremely difficult.
The diversity of transferred components and equipment may drive whether automating all docking and
unloading functions is possible. When it is not possible, glove ports are typically employed to perform the
material transfers. When operations are performed manually using isolator or RABS gloves, it is necessary
to ensure that the glove location and length is suitable for the operations to be carried out. In the case of

--- PDF Page 176 ---
isolators, the position of the gloves must also permit gloves to be extended (without overlap) to perform
biodecontamination, while not obstructing other isolator elements.
RTP canisters and ported bags are established docking technologies. The contents of the canisters and of the
bags must be sterilized with proper technology. Each container is equipped with a port (the beta port) that is
matched to a mating port on the filling enclosure (the alpha port). These two mating surfaces are designed to
connect in a manner that prevents nonsterile external surfaces from being exposed to the components or to
the filling zone. Despite this mating design, if the mating surfaces are not carefully handled and routinely
disinfected, biodecontaminated, or sterilized, a so-called “ring of concern” exists at the juncture of the
mating surfaces that presents a small possibility of exposure when unloading a transport container.
Transport containers or bags must be filled, pretreated, transported, and then attached; because of this,
transport containers are typically limited in size and weight. The transport container contents (e.g.,
components, tools, small equipment parts) are typically positioned loosely for ease of removal; when the
containers are connected and rotated, the components within the load may shift. There are some docking
mechanisms where the ring alone is the rotating section, but this is less common than the type where the
container rotates. Suppliers of transport containers often have proprietary connection designs for the alpha
and beta ports that may not be compatible with the fixed mounting surfaces of the alpha ports already
installed on the equipment. This highlights the importance of coordinating with component suppliers to
ensure that they can match the alpha-port design of the corresponding equipment.
Transport containers are flexible in terms of size, load, and available positioning of various docking ports on
the filling enclosure, because of this they can be used for a wide variety of load elements, including
components (e.g., glassware, stoppers), consumables (e.g., tubing sets, filtration apparatus), or equipment
(e.g., tools, fill pumps, chutes). When using hydrogen peroxide to biodecontaminate transport containers
that include product-contacting materials (e.g., glassware, stoppers, tubing sets for fluid pathway, filling
needles), care must be taken to ensure that residual hydrogen peroxide does not degrade the product through
oxidation. When using rapid cycles for connected transport containers, the residual hydrogen peroxide levels
within the container must be suitable before opening the container to the filling zone where the product will
be exposed. This may be especially important if the load in the container includes polymers that are
hydrogen-peroxide absorptive. PDA has published Points-to-Consider technical documents on oxidation and
on isolators that provide additional detail on handling indirect-contact parts (16, 24).

### 10.3 Conveyance Considerations for Glassware

For nested components, the conveyance system either handles the tub or removes the nest (i.e., the insert
within the tub) that contains the glassware and conveys the nest alone. The tubs and nests are molded plastic
components that are generally highly dimensionally reproducible and offer few challenges in consistency of
conveyance. The amount of bow on the sides of the nest is commonly the only dimension that might be
variable.
Individual glass components that are individually and directly conveyed may present greater handling
challenges. For example, variations in glass dimensions may result in containers that are unstable on the
conveyor or fit inconsistently within feed screws, star wheels, walking beams, or combs. Primary containers,
such as vials or cartridges, may topple at transition points on the conveyor or when back-pressure is reduced
due to a break in production, resulting in the need for downed- or fallen-container sensors or rejection
stations. In general, guided conveyance with feed screws, star wheels, combs, pucks, fitted guiderails, or
other placement devices are better at precision placement under fill-needles than components that are solely

--- PDF Page 177 ---
fed through back-pressure of a line of components through guard rails on a moving conveyor. This may be
an important consideration for small-neck openings or small barrels where centered targeting becomes
critical to prevent filling needle contact with the top or sides of container (which could result in chips on the
sealing surface of the container or a bent needle) and to prevent overspray on the necks or sides of the barrel
(which could lead to improper sealing of elastomers). Overload sensors may be employed to prevent the risk
of damage due to a needle strike on the sealing surface of the container, but does not mitigate all the risks of
not being appropriately centered.
Many filling systems use a combination of back-pressure in some areas, with guided containers under the
filling-needles and stoppering position(s). The areas where backpressure is used, or where accumulation
devices collect large numbers of filled or unfilled components, may increase the risk of scuffing on the
outside of the container. Such scuffing can result in cosmetic container defects or formation of particulate in
the environment, especially after the depyrogenation of components, which can cause container exteriors to
become excessively “sticky” (12). Containers are available with specialized external coatings that can make
them less susceptible to scuffing and fracture if this becomes an excessive issue. Alternatively, “transport”
silicone may be sprayed on the exterior of containers (e.g., cartridges) to reduce the transport friction in the
depyrogenation tunnel and along the line.

### 10.4 Disinfection, Biodecontamination, and Sterilization Associated with

Material Transfer
The more elements associated with the transport system, the greater the risk for cleaning and disinfection or
sterilization to maintain a clean, aseptic environment. If using a disinfectant, such as hydrogen peroxide
within an enclosure, the surface biodecontamination may be easily addressed. On a conventional line,
without the potential for a gas or vapor-phase biodecontamination, disinfection (e.g., nonproduct-contact
parts), or disassembly and sterilization (e.g., direct and indirect product-contact parts) may be required
between batches.
When designing the cycle conditions for hydrogen peroxide within the filling zone, care must be taken to
ensure that hot and cold spots are minimized and the consistent distribution of the gas or vapor-phase
biodecontamination is maintained. This is the case in such areas as motors, cooling coils, autoclave or oven
doors, tunnel-cooling zone surfaces, lyophilization surfaces, or surfaces that are subjected to CIP or SIP.
Care must also be taken with residuals to ensure they are brought to safe levels before any product is
exposed to materials or surfaces that have had contact with the hydrogen peroxide. More detailed
information can be found in PDA points-to-consider technical documents on isolators and oxidation (16,
24).
Tunnels are designed to directly discharge to the filling line; however, glass containers must traverse a
cooling zone within the tunnel so they may enter the filling line at a safe temperature for filling that does not
disrupt the temperature and airflow within the filling line or degrade the product being filled. The tunnel’s
cooling zone must be periodically biodecontaminated (e.g., by heating up for conventional lines, by vapor-
phase for isolators) to maintain the integrity of this transport zone (21).
Steam sterilization within an autoclave can be widely applied to both direct and indirect product-contact
parts. When of appropriate size, parts may be sterilized directly within a transport container. When a
transport container is not in use and the autoclave is not directly connected to the filling enclosure, parts will
require wrapping before being transported to the line. Those parts can then be introduced to the enclosure

--- PDF Page 178 ---
prior to a hydrogen-peroxide cycle if one is being used. Alternatively, multiple layers of wrap or other
secondary sterile packaging applied to the parts can be stripped as the parts approach the critical zone for
installation. This latter approach is no longer favored from a contamination control perspective. Topic 6,
“Material Transport and Loading of Isolators,” of the PDA Points to Consider for the Aseptic Processing of
Sterile Pharmaceutical Products in Isolators identifies additional controls for the introduction of parts to an
isolator when they are too large for transport containers (16).
Sterilization by SIP especially applies for direct product-contact parts, such as pumps and needles, and for
clean media supply, such as water for injection and nitrogen (see Section 2.0).
Dry-heat ovens, like autoclaves, are batch processors. Unlike steam sterilization, however, many polymers
cannot handle the high temperatures associated with dry-heat sterilization or depyrogenation. Consequently,
sterile wraps are not an option for materials to be processed with dry heat. The three options for transfer of
dry-heat processed materials are (1) transport containers with dry-heat-capable alpha-beta ports, (2) metal
trays, or (3) direct connection of the oven to the filling enclosure. The use of metal trays has fallen into
disfavor due to their poor sealing unless a secondary process, such as hydrogen peroxide, can be conducted
after introduction of the parts.
Penetration into filling enclosures represents points of potential ingress of contamination. Therefore, utility
penetrations, glove ports, alpha-ports for docking, and door–hatch seals should be checked routinely for
correct installation (e.g., for those that are removed) and for tightness. The “ring of concern” for alpha and
beta ports should be carefully disinfected or biodecontaminated to reduce contamination risks.
Specialized sterilization processes (e.g., E-Beam, X-ray, EtO, gamma irradiation) and depyrogenation
processes (e.g., chemical degradation, washing) are also possible, but these are often conducted as off-line
processes, with subsequent transport and introduction (see Section 9.0).

--- PDF Page 179 ---

## 11.0 Sterile Filtration and Filling System Considerations

There is no one filling path that will work for every process. The design and integration of a filtration
system with the fluid pathway will result from the consideration of a wider variety of factors that will suit
the product attributes, SA, and risk.

### 11.1 Overview of the Integration of Sterile Filtration with the Filling

System
The integration of the product sterilizing filter for subsequent sterile filling is impacted by various
considerations related to the product, filter positioning in the fluid path, and integrity testing of the fluid
pathway and sterilizing filter. Decisions must also be made on how many filters will be integrated into the
fluid path—one or two (in-line or parallel)—as well as the rationale for the choice.
PDA Technical Report No. 26 (Revised 2025): Sterilizing Filtration of Liquids and two PDA technical
documents—Points to Consider for Risks Associated with Sterilizing Grade Filters and Sterilizing Filtration
and Points to Consider for Implementation of Pre-Use Post-Sterilization Integrity Testing (PUPSIT)—
address the majority of the technical challenges and the integration of sterile filtration into a filling operation
(26-28).
The regulatory expectation is that the final sterilizing filter should be close to the point of filling (3). The
industry recognizes that there are practicalities for how close is “close,” and there is a requirement for risk
assessment when justifying any specific configuration. Prefiltration bioburden reduction will also need to be
considered along the full product pathway, from formulation to the point of filling.
For SUS configured as fluid paths, the PUPSIT considerations include whether the wetting of the filter is via
product or water and, if by water, whether post-integrity-test drying is required. When the fluid path
includes rigid intermediate or header vessels, integrating a vent filter is necessary, as is considering how
such filters will be managed (installed and integrity-tested).
When a nitrogen overlay is required for sensitive products, the gas sterilizing filter, that filter’s integrity, and
the sterility of the gas pathway also need to be considered.
With so many variables involved in the configuration and management of integrity, both for the fluid
pathway and the installed filters, risk assessment is needed. The risk assessment must consider inherent and
corrective interventions and their inclusion in an APS. Additional robust justifications will be required for
any configurations that are contrary to regulatory expectations, presenting appropriate scientific support for
the balance between product risk and benefit.
In some cases, the filling-system design will influence the exact filtration-system design that must be
implemented.

--- PDF Page 180 ---
The key points to consider in designing a fluid-pathway configuration include:
• Product attributes
• Product transfer mechanism (e.g., overpressure vs. pumping)
• Selection and type of filter housing (e.g., capsule vs. cartridge)
• Number of filters and their installation (e.g., parallel installations for redundancies)
• Duration of fill vs. sterile-hold period, if filtering to a bulk intermediate vessel
• Sterilization methodology (e.g., gamma-irradiated SU, autoclave, SIP connection)
• Filter position (inside or outside barrier)
• Integrity testing in place (and its influence on the fluid path)
• PUPSIT implementation considerations
• Fluid-path design (and integration with the filler and sterilization of the fluid path)
• Other filtration requirements for the process (e.g., vessel-vent filters, inert-gas point-of-use filters,
exhaust filtration for DPs)
The design elements must be selected with the goals of fluid transfer in mind, including but not limited to:
• Ensure that the fluid pathway can be sterilized using the selected method (e.g., gamma irradiation,
off-line sterilization, or SIP)
• Ensure that the fluid pathway is capable of the aseptic conveyance of the product end-to-end
• Limit product loss (e.g., during activities such as PUPSIT, priming, conclusion of the fill)
• Limit geometries that could result in shear forces or cause foaming (e.g., pressure drop, sharp angles
or sharp transitions)
• Support required monitoring and control (e.g., temperature, pressure, high-speed cameras for filling
curve monitoring)
• Compatible with product contact (e.g., limited adsorption or absorption, limited extractables and
leachables)

### 11.2 The Influence of Product Attributes on Filtration System Design

One of the first considerations when selecting a filtration system and fluid pathway configuration is to
understand the product attributes. These product attributes will drive the filtration and fluid pathway
configuration including:
• Filter-positioning
• Number of filters
• Integrity-testing
For example, if the product is a solution, there are few limitations to the selected design. Products that
require temperature control, such as cooling throughout the filling operation (including prefill hold times),
may require special considerations to keep the majority of the bulk in a jacketed vessel or cold room to
reduce the time out of temperature. The design considerations might include minimizing line-length of
transfer and filling-fluid pathway while positioning the bulk holding vessel in a location that does not
adversely impact the cleanroom operations (e.g., influence on unidirectional airflow due to temperature
differentials, condensation) while maintaining adequate cooling to the point of fill. Process validation must
ensure that the time out of temperature (whether after thaw-for-frozen product or cooling for refrigerated
products) is maintained within acceptable standards for product quality (see Section 4.7 for additional
considerations for temperature-controlled products).

--- PDF Page 181 ---
The filterability of the product, including such attributes as viscosity and likelihood of product blocking or
blinding the filter, will drive the need to consider whether parallel filter trains or in-line prefilters are needed
to provide redundancy and prevent blockage during the filling operation.
The hazardous nature of the product is another consideration for filtration, whether it is highly potent,
sensitizing, or toxic. Containment and decontamination considerations should be included in the design of
filtration manifolds so that waste associated with PUPSIT or filter-flush at the beginning of processing is
handled in a way that does not risk the employees, the environment, or the outside of any filled containers.

### 11.3 Product Transfer Modalities and Influence on System Design

The two primary methods to get product from a vessel through the filter-manifold, either to another vessel
or directly to the filler, are overpressure and pumping. Overpressure is provided by the application of a
sterile gas (either clean, compressed air or an inert gas such as nitrogen) in the headspace of the pressure
vessel. The increase in pressure in the headspace forces the product through the bottom outlet of the vessel
and through the transfer lines. The pressure can be controlled to either continuously or intermittently feed
the product. Pumping is the application of a mechanical transfer of the fluid in the transfer line. The pump
can be product-contacting (e.g., rotary lobe, centrifugal) or non-product-contacting (e.g., peristaltic). Care
must be taken to ensure that, with either transfer mechanism, the pressure and any pulsation of the liquids
does not harm the filter membrane or create shear stress on the product that can result in the formation of
particulate or harm-filterable suspensions for example.
Configurations for transfer can include presterilized intermediate vessels (also known as surge vessels) in
conjunction with a manifold, or the transfer can be made directly to the filler manifold without an
intermediate vessel. The filler manifold assists in securing a stable flow of product to the filling pumps and
needles by providing a small upstream reservoir or widening of the fluid pathway. Table 11.3-1 lists some
of the considerations for these different configurations.

--- PDF Page 181 ---
The filterability of the product, including such attributes as viscosity and likelihood of product blocking or
blinding the filter, will drive the need to consider whether parallel filter trains or in-line prefilters are needed
to provide redundancy and prevent blockage during the filling operation.
The hazardous nature of the product is another consideration for filtration, whether it is highly potent,
sensitizing, or toxic. Containment and decontamination considerations should be included in the design of
filtration manifolds so that waste associated with PUPSIT or filter-flush at the beginning of processing is
handled in a way that does not risk the employees, the environment, or the outside of any filled containers.

### 11.3 Product Transfer Modalities and Influence on System Design

The two primary methods to get product from a vessel through the filter-manifold, either to another vessel
or directly to the filler, are overpressure and pumping. Overpressure is provided by the application of a
sterile gas (either clean, compressed air or an inert gas such as nitrogen) in the headspace of the pressure
vessel. The increase in pressure in the headspace forces the product through the bottom outlet of the vessel
and through the transfer lines. The pressure can be controlled to either continuously or intermittently feed
the product. Pumping is the application of a mechanical transfer of the fluid in the transfer line. The pump
can be product-contacting (e.g., rotary lobe, centrifugal) or non-product-contacting (e.g., peristaltic). Care
must be taken to ensure that, with either transfer mechanism, the pressure and any pulsation of the liquids
does not harm the filter membrane or create shear stress on the product that can result in the formation of
particulate or harm-filterable suspensions for example.
Configurations for transfer can include presterilized intermediate vessels (also known as surge vessels) in
conjunction with a manifold, or the transfer can be made directly to the filler manifold without an
intermediate vessel. The filler manifold assists in securing a stable flow of product to the filling pumps and
needles by providing a small upstream reservoir or widening of the fluid pathway. Table 11.3-1 lists some
of the considerations for these different configurations.

--- PDF Page 182 ---

**Table 11.3-1 Transfer System Design Influences on Filtration Risks**

Method of Filter Sterile Filler Filler Considerations
Transfer from Intermediate Manifold
Formulation Vessel
Vessel
Overpressure Yes No Yes Consider pressure drop across the filter membrane, as filling system
controls the rate of transfer for time-pressure filling systems. Under
certain conditions, if stable pressure on the dosing system cannot be
maintained, the dosing accuracy could be affected.
Overpressure Yes Yes Yes Rate of transfer controlled by filtration operation alone; limited risk for
pressure drop.

filter membrane, as the pump will need to be interlocked with the
filler. Under certain conditions, if stable pressure on the dosing system
cannot be maintained, the dosing accuracy could be affected.
Pump Yes Yes Yes Rate of transfer controlled by the filtration operation alone to the
intermediate vessel; offers less risk of start/stop to the filter
membrane.

### 11.4 Filter Selection and Influences on System Design

The two primary filter types are cartridge filters and capsule filters. Cartridge filters are filter membranes installed within a filter housing that is part of
the equipment. O-rings at the bottom of the filter create the seal with the equipment housing. Cartridge filters can be either sterilized out of place in an
autoclave or SIP on a rigid piping system, and then integrity-tested in place (see Section 11.9).
Capsule filters are supplied with a molded-polymer housing that is an integral part of the supplied filter. The housing, which is pressure-rated, is
equipped with connectors and test connections that can be clamped directly to existing stainless-steel transfer lines, creating a continuous pathway that
can be CIP, SIP, or assembled and autoclaved. The capsule filter is then integrity-tested according to the PUPSIT justification (see Section 11.9). The
selection of the type of filter to be used is driven in part by the complexity of connections and complexity of the equipment for the filtration-unit
operation. Capsule filters are more highly integrated with the fluid pathway and are considered the filter of choice for SUS.

--- PDF Page 183 ---
The materials of construction of the transfer lines, filter, and filter-housing should be evaluated for
compatibility with the product to prevent the risk, for example, API or preservative adsorption, oxidation, or
exposure to free radicals. When any of these conditions are unavoidable, consideration should be given to
the flush quantities required to standardize the product homogeneity (e.g., through binding to available
sites). This flush quantity must be proven through validation, and the line length and filter surface area must
be tightly controlled and standardized by procedure.

### 11.5 Filter Configuration Alternatives

A wide variety of filter configurations are possible. The product attributes will drive the selection of the
filter configuration, as will considerations for risk mitigation associated with SA. The examples of filter
configuration for the fluid pathways depicted in Figure 11.9.2-1 through Figure 11.9.2-3, below are just
examples; each manufacturer must determine the best approach to filtration by balancing the product
requirements and risks.
One common approach is the installation of a bioburden-reduction filter placed upstream of the sterilizing
filter. In some cases, two sterilizing filters can be placed in a series; this ensures that, if the downstream
filter fails an integrity test, the upstream filter may be tested for integrity. If the upstream filter passes, then
the batch disposition is not compromised.
Another common configuration is the installation of two parallel fluid pathways, each with a sterilizing filter
in place. In this redundant parallel configuration, the process would proceed through the first pathway, with
pressure being monitored throughout, until a pressure rise is observed across the membrane. When that
pressure rise is observed, the fluid pathway is switched to the secondary pathway. This approach is often
used when working with products that are likely to block the filter due to particulate or product deposition
that blocks the filter’s pores (e.g., large molecules, proteinaceous, long filtration times due to large batches
or prolonged filling operations). Legacy configurations that employ the removal of a prefilter during
processing, rather than employing a redundant filtration manifold, should be assessed for compliance;
breaking into a transfer line and filtration manifold after integrity-testing presents a significant risk (see
Section 11.9).

### 11.6 Duration of Fill and Impact on Filtration System Layout

When considering the configuration for the fluid path and whether to make use of a sterile holding vessel,
the duration of the filling process and the likely duration of the filtration process should be taken into
account. The duration of the filtration is an important parameter in the initial qualification of the filter
membrane and one that will require requalification should the filtration time be changed over the life of the
product (26).
Two failure modes are possible if the filling process is prolonged. First, as the bulk product is held during
presterilization, the bioburden may replicate in the nonsterile bulk intermediate vessel and, as a result,
present a challenge to the sterilizing filter. Second, there is a risk of “grow through” for the filter, where
microorganisms on the upstream side of the filter can divide and multiply, grow through the membrane, and
breach the intended sterile barrier of the filter membrane. Consequently, if the filling process is prolonged,
thereby prolonging the filtration process, consideration should be given to using a sterile transfer vessel so
the filtration can be accomplished more quickly. This approach will require additional space for the
placement of the sterile vessel. The presterilized intermediate vessel must be held under positive pressure
after sterilization and throughout the filling operation. In addition, the sterile intermediate vessel will require

--- PDF Page 184 ---
a sterilizing vent-filter during the transfer process, which will also require an integrity test at the conclusion
of the filling process. Possible configurations for the fluid pathway are illustrated in Figure 11.9.2-1, Figure
11.9.2-2, and Figure 11.9.2-3. The sterile hold-time for the bulk must be validated as part of the
manufacturing process. Other possible consequences of prolonged filling operations include blocking of the
sterilizing filter, as discussed in Section 11.5.
For direct filtration without a bulk intermediate vessel, slow and fast fill-speeds must be validated to ensure
that the filtration parameters (e.g., maximum and minimum flow rate, differential pressure) can be
maintained while supplying the filler with consistent product flow.
Aseptic filling operations are expected to have point-of-use filtration, regardless of the filling system design,
when permitted by the product and filler attributes (see Section 11.11).

### 11.7 Sterilization of the Filter and Filter Train

When selecting a sterilizing filter, there are multiple options for ensuring the sterility of the filter and its
associated transfer lines. Filters can be gamma-irradiated SU, or they can be sterilized on site, either through
an autoclave load or SIP cycles.
For optimal SA, all connections required for the filter should be made prior to the sterilization step,
especially on the downstream side; as an alternative, intrinsic sterile connection-devices should be
considered to reduce the risk of sterility breaches during assembly. These approaches would require
coordination with the filter supplier to ensure that the needed connections can be supplied if the filter will
arrive RTU. For on-site filter assembly, there may be more flexibility in the connectors used, as bioburden-
controlled assembly can be implemented prior to the sterilization step.
The mechanism of transfer to the line should be considered, as it will determine whether the filtration
apparatus requires special packaging, such as double- or triple-bagging for introduction to the filling area, or
whether a RTP system or other advanced protective measures will be used (see Section 9.0 and Section
10.0 for the transfer of materials and components).
The method of sterilization should also be evaluated for impact on the product. For example, when using
gamma-irradiation, the formation of free radicals may result in the need to reject the first quantity of filtered
product as a precautionary measure (see Section 12.0 for information on discard considerations).

### 11.8 Overview of Factors Influencing Filtration-Pathway Layout

When considering the overall fluid pathway for the product to reach the filler, the location of the filter is an
important design aspect. For SA, the ideal position is to place the filter as close as possible to the filling
system to minimize the sterile fluid pathway. However, there are many interactions with the filter train that
should be considered to ensure that there are adequate provisions for access and interface without risk of
contamination to the filling system, such as:
• Setup connections
• PUPSIT
• Initial venting of the filter
• Drying the filter, when required, prior to or after integrity-testing
• Start-up rejection volumes
• Need to change over to a parallel filter installation during the process, when used

--- PDF Page 185 ---
The shortest fluid pathway is often placement of the filter within the aseptic boundary, such as inside an
isolator, as seen in Figure 11.9.2-1. However, this positioning will require a mechanism, such as a transfer
port, to introduce the filter to the aseptic environment and additional interventions within the aseptic
boundary for setup, testing (e.g., PUPSIT), and changeover. Placement of the filter within the aseptic
boundary can also result in other challenges to maintaining the aseptic environment (e.g., airflow impacts,
additional mechanical components within the aseptic boundary that require maintenance or calibration,
cleaning and disinfection due to additional complexity). As a result, many configurations now call for
placement of the filter outside the sterile boundary with a sterile connector to the fluid pathway (see Figure
11.9.2-2 and Figure 11.9.2-3).
With bulk solutions that require mixing or recirculation during filling, such as filterable suspensions, the
placement of the filter requires a number of considerations (see Section 4.6):
• Filtration on demand for products that require recirculation may result in the need to clear the filter
after any period of flow hold; filtration to a sterile intermediate vessel with recirculation capability
can reduce the risk of settling during filtration.
• Use of sterile intermediate vessels and recirculation loops increase the distance between the
sterilizing filter and the point of fill, which will require risk assessment.

### 11.9 Integrity Testing and PUPSIT Implementation

The EU Annex 1 expectation for integrity-testing is that it will occur in place, that is, in the filter housing
(3). The expectation for any filter installed in a removable housing is that they will be tested together in their
installed position, not off-line with the housing removed. All required connections and valving should be
included in the filling system and fluid-pathway design to prevent unnecessary connections during setup and
testing (27, 28).

#### 11.9.1 PUPSIT Implementation Considerations

The EU Annex 1 requires that filters be integrity-tested both before and after use (3). If pre-use testing
represents a risk to the product or process, a suitable justification must be available in the quality-system
documentation to demonstrate why the pretest will not be performed.
One important PUPSIT criteria is whether the filter will be integrity-wetted and -tested with product or with
water for injection (WFI) at the pre- and post-use test points. When using WFI as a pretest material, the
filter must be installed in a manner that the filter and the fluid pathway are easily drainable. The filter is
typically dried with sterile compressed-air or nitrogen or is flushed with sufficient product after testing to
ensure that there is no risk of compromise or dilution of the product. (The introduction of valving and
utility-piping for the drying medium should be part of the overall piping configuration when used.) When
using WFI as a post-test material, the product must be thoroughly rinsed from the membrane with sufficient
WFI to ensure a valid measurement. When using product to test the filter before and after use, a small
amount of product may be lost; however, the preflush requirements for leachables and extractables may
include the amount consumed during a pretest.
In all cases, the WFI or product used for the filter integrity-testing must have a mechanism for disposal that
does not contaminate the filling line. For hazardous products, care must be taken to ensure appropriate
containment. This may require a biocontainment bag or waste system to be employed with appropriate
valving and connectors to protect both operators and the environment.

--- PDF Page 186 ---
The design of the assembly should ensure appropriate valves are in place for management of the fluid
pathway. In some cases, the valves may be automated with confirmation of positioning. In other cases, the
valves may be entirely manual; in that case, detailed procedures must be available to ensure the correct
sequential operation of the valves.
To ensure consistent configuration of the tubing sets, a shadow board displaying the appropriate outlines
and providing the needed hooks that represent the desired configuration could be used by the operator to lay
out the tubing and connectors during assembly prior to sterilization.

#### 11.9.2 Layout Alternatives for the Filter and Sterile Fluid-Pathway

Multiple fluid-path configurations are possible that consider the various requirements related to product,
assembly, integrity, position of the sterilizing filter, and associated header- and hold-vessels or bags. Figure
11.9.2-1, Figure 11.9.2-2, and Figure 11.9.2-3 depict diverse arrangements that represent just a few of the
many possible configurations for the sterile fluid pathway. The advantages and disadvantages of each
illustrated configuration have been provided to help illustrate possible considerations for the numerous of
other possible layouts. In all cases, the risk-benefit analysis should be part of an appropriate risk assessment
that can be used to support the site’s aseptic-process control strategy and the overall CCS.

*[Figure 11.9.2-1 depicts a configuration where the filter(s) is located inside the barrier. A single aseptic]*

connection is made in the surrounding environment through the isolator or barrier wall to the upstream side
of the filter, which permits connection to the sterile fluid path. The sterile intermediate bag downstream of
the filter that helps maintain fill accuracy is also present inside of the barrier (see Section 9.0 and Section
10.0). The advantage of this configuration is that the filter is maintained within the aseptic environment,
providing the highest level of SA, while all connections are made on the nonsterile side of the filter. The
primary disadvantage of this arrangement is that the complexity of the interventions for setup and integrity-
testing add increased risk that can compromise the aseptic environment. While it is possible to bring
presterilized and preassembled components in through a RTP, there may be size restrictions. Alternatively,
the individual components may be brought in, subjected to biodecontamination within the environment
(e.g., in hydrogen peroxide-impermeable packaging), and then assembled with dedicated and specific
connections (as recommended by EU Annex I). However, without the benefit of a shadow board to aid in
assembly, the risk of operator error increases.
While the sterile holding-vessel in Figure 11.9.2-1 is located within the barrier in a protected position (an
advantage), adequate space must be made within the barrier for the placement of this bag (a possible
disadvantage). This may also limit the practical size of the sterile holding-vessel.
In Figure 11.9.2-1, Figure 11.9.2-2, and Figure 11.9.2-3, the filter is depicted as a single unit for the
simplicity of drawing. However, it is possible to have more than one filter in a series or more than one
filtration setup in parallel; all connection points would then be multiplied accordingly. In addition, the
complexity of the PUPSIT-valve assembly is depicted as a single icon when, in reality, it is much more
complex, with test, flush, and drying connections. Pressure monitoring is typically located upstream of the
filter, however configurations can vary with between one and three monitoring points possible, depending
upon the process risk assessment, the exact configuration of the piping and the number of filters. Therefore,
pressure monitoring has also not been depicted, but is an essential part of the design (i.e., to monitor the
differential pressure across the filter membrane). In addition, if the vessels are stainless steel, each vessel
will require a vent filter (not shown); SU bags do not typically require a vent connection for filtration and
transfer.

--- PDF Page 187 ---

*[Figure 11.9.2-1 Filter Position within the Aseptic Barrier]*

*[Figure 11.9.2-2 depicts a configuration where the filter(s) is located outside the barrier. An intrinsic sterile]*

connection device is used to make a connection to the fluid pathway. The connection is made downstream
of the sterilizing filter, outside the Grade-A zone. The sterile intermediate vessel in Figure 11.9.2-2
downstream of the filter is also present outside the barrier. (It is possible to also move this sterile
connection, and even the intermediate vessel, to the inside of the aseptic barrier.) The primary advantage of
the configuration shown in Figure 11.9.2-2 is the access to and simplicity of making the connections
required for integrity-testing, which may include the use of a shadow board. The primary disadvantage of
this configuration is the integrity risk, as there is a significant portion of the sterile fluid pathway positioned
in the environment outside the aseptic barrier. As such, the integrity from the filter to the point of use must
be assured. Testing of the fluid pathway, not just integrity-testing of the filter assembly, will be required to
ensure that sterility is maintained.

*[Figure 11.9.2-2 Filter Position Outside of the Aseptic Barrier]*

--- PDF Page 188 ---

*[Figure 11.9.2-3 is a slight variation on Figure 11.9.2-2, one that uses a header bag near the point of fill. All]*

of the other advantages and disadvantages from Figure 11.9.2-2 also apply to Figure 11.9.2-3. The header
bag represents an additional point of connection and an extension of the sterile fluid pathway that must be
maintained as integral. The header bag can improve the filling precision; however, it may require an
additional supply pump, usually positioned outside the barrier, to transfer the product to the header bag. The
configuration introduces additional complexity to the sterile fluid pathway, posing an added risk to the
integrity of the overall system. The benefit in filling accuracy must outweigh this risk, and whether there are
other approaches to improve the filling accuracy (e.g., change in filling technology, position of intermediate
vessel, optimization of filling setup such as hose diameter and filling-needle geometry, optimization of
filling curve) should be explored. Overpressure, as a means of product transfer, would not be recommended
on the sterile product vessel upstream of the header bag as it could become an impediment to the sterile
filtration.

*[Figure 11.9.2-3 Filter Position Outside the Aseptic Barrier with Header Bag]*

When the bulk is required to be held in a cold room due to temperature restrictions on the product, the
activities depicted outside of the barrier would commonly be held in the cold room, with or without a sterile
product vessel (see Section 4.7 for additional details on needed controls for handling temperature-sensitive
products, as other process requirements could also apply).
Regardless of filter positioning, some general design considerations apply to all configurations. The fluid
transfer-line diameter should be as small as possible and the length as short as possible while still providing
the necessary flow rates to the process. Doing so will reduce the amount of scrapped product due to line
loss. Other product risk factors, such as shear force, should also be considered when determining line
dimensions in order to reduce friction during transfer under pressure. The best balance of the two conditions,
that is, line loss and shear force, should be sought.

### 11.10 Aseptic Filling Technology Influence on Filter and Fluid Pathway

Configuration
Table 11.10-1 summarizes equipment-specific designs that might affect the selection of the fluid pathway
beyond the selection of the process filter that should be considered, such as:

--- PDF Page 189 ---
• For rolling DPs, the pump itself may cause some pulsation (depending on design and size), which
presents a higher risk to the filter membrane. Increasing the distance between the filter and the
pump may dampen this effect.
• For RPPs, there is a risk of back-flow through the filter if the volume (e.g., manifold or reservoir)
between the pump and the filter is inadequate.
• Having a manifold (header) upstream of the filling heads (e.g., filling pumps for RPP or DP, pinch
valves in TP filling systems, rotors in PP filling systems) is a common design feature. When the
filter is positioned to provide point-of-use filtration, a small dead-leg can be formed between the
filter and the end of the manifold. This dead-leg can create a risk when performing such activities as
filter-flushing at the beginning of the filling operation. The dead-leg provides some uncertainty
about whether the full volume of the waste solution has been expelled from the system.
• For RPP and TP filling systems, including a mechanism that vents air from the filling system should
be considered. For example, air removal is beneficial at startup, as the manifold first fills, or when
there are interventions that introduce air to the system. If the manifold can be positioned below the
filling pumps, this positioning may reduce or eliminate the need for a separate venting mechanism,
if air can escape through the filling heads.
• Design of the manifold should be carefully considered to ensure that it can be appropriately
sterilized and assembled in the fluid pathway in an aseptic manner, especially when introducing the
manifold through a RTP for assembly. If it is subject to SIP, the manifold represents a widening of
the line and a transition to multiple filling systems, so it is imperative to ensure that an adequate
mechanism for complete air removal during sterilization is present.

In addition to these considerations for in-line filtration to liquid filling systems, other filters may be in place
along the fluid pathway, including:
• Vessel vent filters (not required for SU bags)
• Inert-gas point-of-use filters
• Manifold/filling-head venting system filters (as applicable)
Integrity-testing will be required for each of these additional process filters. That integrity-testing will
commonly be performed off-line as these are hydrophobic filters that would commonly be tested with
alcohol.

### 11.11 Considerations Regarding Products that are Not Filterable

Some products cannot be sterile-filtered, for example, some suspension products and cell therapy products
where the particle size of the suspension or cells exceeds the porosity rating of the filter. Terminal
sterilization should always be the first choice for the preparation of sterile products. However, where neither
terminal sterilization nor sterile filtration are possible due to the composition and attributes of the product, a
control strategy must be in place. The product and process control strategy must address the steps taken to
ensure the sterility of each ingredient/component of the product, including the potential for filtration of each
prior to introduction to the unfilterable component, and to ensure that the procedures used to bring the
various ingredients/components together provide the highest possible SA. Minimizing the length of the fluid
pathway and ensuring its integrity after sterilization are essential elements to success for these products. A
risk assessment is recommended to support the robustness of the overall control strategy for SA. In these
cases, the process is the product; that is, the combination of all the sterile inputs provides the assurance of
sterility for the final product. Hence, each must be separately assured.

### 11.12 Filtration Associated with Powder Filling

Although there is no filtration associated with a product in powder-filling, filtration is associated with:
• Powder delivery and handling
• Inerting
• Drying after cleaning
• Exhaust and dust collection
For vacuum pressure powder fillers (VPPF), point-of-use filtration is used for the clean compressed air and
the vacuum. The filters are installed between the utility source (e.g., vacuum, dosing air, purge air) and the
mechanical backplate or the pneumatic rotary joint. (For fillers designed for dosing needles, there is one
filter per air or vacuum pathway.) The filter assembly is installed as part of the aseptic setup of the filling
system.
For both VPPF and auger fillers (AF), an inert gas or clean compressed air may also be introduced to assist
with powder flow within the hopper. For both VPPF and AF, if the product requires protection from oxygen,
a filtered inerting-gas is also used during the filling operation.
For AF, no clean compressed air or vacuum is associated with the filling operation; however, filtered clean
compressed air is associated with drying the powder pathway after cleaning. If CIP is associated with the
bulk-hopper component of the VPPF filler, the bulk hopper would also be subject to drying with filtered
clean compressed air. (VPPF dosing-system elements, other than the bulk hopper, are not capable of CIP
and, therefore, will not have clean compressed air for system drying.)

--- PDF Page 191 ---
All filter usage for VPPF and AF described here make use of a sterilizing-grade hydrophobic filter with a
nominal pore size of 0.22 µm. These filters are typically procured as sterile, gamma-irradiated capsule
filters, although autoclavable options may also be used. All piping downstream of the filter should be
capable of sterilization. All filters should be subject to integrity-testing, and the integrity-test results should
be considered in the batch disposition process.
Either filler type may make use of dust collection, which would use HEPA-filtered exhaust at the point of
collection. These filters are generally located within a gray space to facilitate change-out of the filter and
emptying of the reservoir outside the cleanroom environment; however, cleanroom models do exist. Dust-
collection systems may also employ a bag-in–bag-out configuration for potent compounds that require
containment. These HEPA filters are intended for containment rather than microbiological control;
therefore, the frequency of integrity-testing is at the discretion of the manufacturer based on application,
system design (e.g., location of the filter), and risk.

--- PDF Page 192 ---

## 12.0 Discard Strategies for Aseptic Filling of Liquids

The selection of a filling technology and its specific system design can influence the discard strategies that
are employed for such line events as startup, priming, line stoppage, interventions, and end of the aseptic
fill-unit operation. Products that are very expensive or limited in availability will be impacted
disproportionately by systems that have high discard requirements. Therefore, careful consideration of the
system design and the integration of the filling technology with the filling enclosure/isolator is warranted.

### 12.1 Discard Strategies at Startup

Product rejection at the start of the filling operation, immediately after setup, is not uncommon and may
occur for several reasons. It may be associated with the product formulation, the product’s physicochemical
characteristics, the upstream process, or the filling equipment or it could be due to standardizing the dose of
the product.
Several common causes of container or product rejection include:
• Interaction with the product (e.g., adsorption of API, excipients, preservatives) for tubing or other
polymers (e.g., filters) or elastomers (e.g., diaphragms, gaskets)
• Degradation of the product due to first exposure to surfaces (e.g., free-radical exposure associated
with gamma-irradiated components, oxidation of product within tubing/manifolds) (24)
• Leachables entering the product from first product exposure to surfaces (e.g., filters, transfer lines,
SU intermediate bags)
• Dilution of the product arising from filter integrity-testing (e.g., PUPSIT) or initial filter-flushing if
these tasks are not performed with product
Priming of the filling stations may be required to ensure:
• Elimination of air
• Elimination of foam
• Homogenization (e.g., suspension products)
• Dose standardization (e.g., fill-weight/volume)
• Seating (e.g., relaxation) of tubing within peristaltic pumps
The quantity of product/number of units to be rejected due to these influences should be established prior to
process validation through careful evaluation of content uniformity and fill accuracy. Then, the discard
quantities should be standardized for use in the process validation and in subsequent routine filling. Any
changes to the upstream process involving polymers/elastomers in use, or changes to filling conditions,
could require reevaluation of rejection quantities.

--- PDF Page 193 ---

### 12.2 Discard Strategies at Priming

Some fillers are equipped with a priming feature that is linked to a discard mode for the units processed
while in this mode. Other systems are designed to permit rejection directly to a discard vessel. Fillers may
establish the quantity to be discarded based on the dose volume/weight, the number of priming cycles, or
both.
For high-value products, it may be possible to start the filler in phases to ensure the number of completely
filled containers is maximized. For example, filling can take place directly on the weigh-cells to avoid the
occurrence of partially filled containers during the priming procedure. This approach eliminates the need to
discard containers used during priming, as they are assured to be filled appropriately (see Section 5.0). This
approach requires that other possible priming quality concerns (e.g., adsorption, oxidation, leachables) have
been addressed.
Some filling systems are equipped with a dedicated refilling pump as well as a dedicated weighing system
that permits accurate refilling of any under-filled units associated with the priming stage. The equipment
needs to be designed to properly handle this separate filling path during production.
In all cases, the performance of this priming mode must be challenged during operational qualification to
ensure that the filler is rejecting any units that are improperly filled, in accordance with the intended system
design. Product-development data may be leveraged to ensure that all product attributes have been
accounted for when determining the discard quantities.

### 12.3 Discard Strategies During Filling and Interventions

During the filling operation, interruptions that stop the line (e.g., gaps in component supply, interventions,
operator breaks) or are caused by unexpected events (e.g., particle excursion, loss of temperature in the
tunnel, breakage, fallen units, collisions on the lyophilizer loader) may require rejections. While many
filling-equipment designs have accumulation capability that creates a buffer to prevent stoppages or
minimize their impact, stoppages may still occur. Depending on their duration, these stoppages may result
in:
• Loss in homogeneity (e.g., suspensions)
• Changes in binding to elastomers or polymers (e.g., loss of API, preservative, excipients)
• Loss of headspace or oxidation for oxygen-sensitive products
• Drying at the tip of a needle that causes plugging
• Dripping and loss of weight control (if not 100% IPC)
• Loss of temperature control for products that are cold-filled (e.g., 2–8 °C)
• Risk of compromising containers due to exposure to intervention activities
These, and other similar filling interruptions, may result in the need to reject units. In the case of oxygen-
sensitive products, for instance, any open containers may need to be rejected, in addition to the possible
need to reprime the filler and reject bulk solution associated with the repriming. The number of
units/volume or weight rejected after a line stoppage of a specified time period should be established prior to
process validation through equipment validation and product testing. The requirements for stoppages should
then be included in the site SOP.
Line interventions associated with the filling system itself also may require the discard of product or product
containers. In an optimal line-design, the mechanical components of the system are installed outside the

--- PDF Page 194 ---
filling enclosure (e.g., isolator, RABS), so adjustments can be made in an area that is not proximate to the
open containers and would minimize the risk of introducing contamination to either empty or filled
containers. Some filling designs have clear advantages in enabling the mechanical equipment to be installed
outside of the enclosure, minimizing interventions within the enclosure. The PP tubing, for example, is a
closed system; therefore, PPs can easily be installed remotely from the filling needles. The minor downside
of any technology installed outside the barrier is that the tubing leading to each filling needle represents a
separate penetration to the enclosure. If a leak occurs or an adjustment is needed, an intervention within the
enclosure may still be required. Such an approach, however, is significantly less of a sterility-assurance risk
compared to a system where the majority of the mechanical components are inside the enclosure.
Advancements continue to be made in system design as the industry recognizes the SA advantage in
providing remote mechanical components (see Section 2.0).
Interventions that occur within the filling enclosure may require significant line clearance for components
and for filled, unsealed containers present in the zone where the intervention is to occur. With some systems,
an automated clearance mode is available that will stop the infeed to the filler and reject all containers
within the conveyor system in front of the filler. With legacy systems, where the rejection is entirely manual
care must be taken to perform the intervention using appropriate aseptic techniques, so as to not cause any
product spills and discarding each rejected container with a sterile tool. If there are filled, fully sealed
containers within the line-clearance zone, the decision to reject or retain those containers must be based on
appropriate risk assessment and documented following intervention SOPs at the site.
For oxygen-sensitive products, inert gas overlay (e.g., nitrogen) may need to be established to purge the air
from the headspace of the container. Units with excess oxygen in the headspace may need to be rejected at
the point of headspace-analysis of the containers or at the conclusion of filling. Any failure in the inert-gas
headspace controls during the filling operation (e.g., when the filler is shut down) will result in the rejection
of impacted units during the processing.

### 12.4 Discard Strategies Associated with Dose Control

The loss of dose control is another reason units may be rejected. In this case, the mechanism for weight
verification will influence the number of needed rejections. If 100% weight verification is employed, only
the affected containers will be discarded (see Section 2.0 and Section 5.0). If statistical weight control is
employed, units back to the last known correct weight check will need to be rejected. Filling technology
does not typically affect the discard strategy, although the type and degree of intervention required to correct
and recover the dose control will vary. Any intervention that results in the loss of prime will require not only
the affected containers during the intervention to be discarded, but also any containers associated with the
new priming activity.
With containers of narrow-diameter openings, targeting the delivery of the dose to make sure product is not
sprayed on the neck opening or down the barrel of a syringe or cartridge is important to ensure that
elastomers form effective seals with the container. Any evidence of improper dose-targeting during the
filling operation could also result in rejection.

--- PDF Page 195 ---

### 12.5 Discard Strategies Associated with Conclusion of Fill

At the end of the aseptic fill-unit operation, as bulk product volume decreases, fillers can encounter a loss of
fill accuracy. This may be due to:
• Low levels in the filling manifold/intermediate vessel
• Foam or air introduced while the final volume of product is pumped or pressurized through the filter
or transferred from the bulk vessel
• Loss of homogeneity due to loss of recirculation or mixing capability from low volumes, especially
for suspension products
Fillers may receive automated signals from the manifold or intermediate vessel to start and stop transfer
from the bulk vessel. When the alarm is triggered due to the lack of available bulk (e.g., low or low-low-
level alarm), the filler is directed to begin shut down or initiate line clearance or empty mode. For high-
value products, the shutdown may occur in stages. For example, individual filling paths will be shut down to
reduce the number of filling paths that remain active to complete the fill. Eventually arriving at the point
where all filling paths are shut down, and all partially filled containers are discarded.
The same technology used to permit refilling, to ensure every container is filled properly during priming,
can also be employed to ensure all aseptic end-of-fill unit-operation containers are appropriately filled (see
Section 2.0). The company must establish procedures to handle the end of fill consistently to ensure that
only good-quality containers are produced. These end-of-fill procedures must be included in the validation
of the process (see Section 13.0 for more information on the end of fill).

### 12.6 Discard Strategies Associated with Nested Components

Filling syringes that are nested in tubs can pose discard challenges when the dose-control performance fails
on an individual filling-needle position. Discarding the affected doses is the minimum action required. This
can be accomplished through one of two approaches: Discard individual syringes from a failing
pump/needle or discard the full tub. The discard of individual syringes can be significantly more
complicated as it will require some form of verification, either robotically (e.g., through reject
marking/tracking or through physical segregation) or by employing the four-eyes principle (i.e., independent
verification step) to ensure that all the affected syringes are discarded. (It should also be noted that some
filling technologies do not easily permit the deactivation of a single filling station; this is especially true in
nested configurations. Reset of the entire line may be more appropriate should a fault occur in a single
filling station for these systems.) Discard of the entire tub is a more conservative, and perhaps simpler,
approach from the standpoint of the quality system and the documentation that will be required.
Filling lines that permit the return of units for re-dosing or which employ re-stoppering, technology can be
employed to reduce the number of discards (see Section 5.0 and Section 7.3).
Table 12.6-1 provides some generalizations about the relative risk of different discard events based on the
type of filling technology employed.

--- PDF Page 181 (doc p167) ---
The filterability of the product, including such attributes as viscosity and likelihood of product blocking or
blinding the filter, will drive the need to consider whether parallel filter trains or in-line prefilters are needed
to provide redundancy and prevent blockage during the filling operation.
The hazardous nature of the product is another consideration for filtration, whether it is highly potent,
sensitizing, or toxic. Containment and decontamination considerations should be included in the design of
filtration manifolds so that waste associated with PUPSIT or filter-flush at the beginning of processing is
handled in a way that does not risk the employees, the environment, or the outside of any filled containers.

### 11.3 Product Transfer Modalities and Influence on System Design

The two primary methods to get product from a vessel through the filter-manifold, either to another vessel
or directly to the filler, are overpressure and pumping. Overpressure is provided by the application of a
sterile gas (either clean, compressed air or an inert gas such as nitrogen) in the headspace of the pressure
vessel. The increase in pressure in the headspace forces the product through the bottom outlet of the vessel
and through the transfer lines. The pressure can be controlled to either continuously or intermittently feed
the product. Pumping is the application of a mechanical transfer of the fluid in the transfer line. The pump
can be product-contacting (e.g., rotary lobe, centrifugal) or non-product-contacting (e.g., peristaltic). Care
must be taken to ensure that, with either transfer mechanism, the pressure and any pulsation of the liquids
does not harm the filter membrane or create shear stress on the product that can result in the formation of
particulate or harm-filterable suspensions for example.
Configurations for transfer can include presterilized intermediate vessels (also known as surge vessels) in
conjunction with a manifold, or the transfer can be made directly to the filler manifold without an
intermediate vessel. The filler manifold assists in securing a stable flow of product to the filling pumps and
needles by providing a small upstream reservoir or widening of the fluid pathway. Table 11.3-1 lists some
of the considerations for these different configurations.

--- PDF Page 182 (doc p168) ---

| Method of Transfer | Filter from Formulation Vessel | Sterile Intermediate Vessel | Filler Manifold | Considerations |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Overpressure | Yes | No | Yes | Consider pressure drop across the filter membrane, as filling system controls the rate of transfer for time-pressure filling systems. Under certain conditions, if stable pressure on the dosing system cannot be maintained, the dosing accuracy could be affected. |
| Overpressure | Yes | Yes | Yes | Rate of transfer controlled by filtration operation alone; limited risk for pressure drop. |
| Pump | Yes | No | Yes | Start/stop of the pump should be considered relative to the risk to the filter membrane, as the pump will need to be interlocked with the filler. Under certain conditions, if stable pressure on the dosing system cannot be maintained, the dosing accuracy could be affected. |
| Pump | Yes | Yes | Yes | Rate of transfer controlled by the filtration operation alone to the intermediate vessel; offers less risk of start/stop to the filter membrane. |

certain conditions, if stable pressure on the dosing system cannot be
maintained, the dosing accuracy could be affected.

pressure drop.
